Generics with Wildcard pattern: 
Generic methods with wildcard pattern 
================================= 
? => it is a wild chard symbol to indicate any type i can 
collect. 
methodOne(ArrayList<String> l): 
This method is applicable for ArrayList of only String 
type. 
Example: 
l.add("A"); 
l.add(null); 
l.add(10);//(invalid) 
Within the method we can add only String type of 
objects and null to the List. 
methodOne(ArrayList<?> l): 
We can use this method for ArrayList of any type but 
within the method we can't add 
anything to the List except null. 
Example: 
l.add(null);//(valid) 
l.add("A");//(invalid) 
l.add(10);//(invalid) 
This method is useful whenever we are performing 
only read operation. 
methodOne(ArrayList<? Extends x> l): 
If x is a class then this method is applicable for ArrayList of either x type or its child classes. 
If x is an interface then this method is applicable for ArrayList of either x type or its implementation 
classes. 
In this case also within the method we can't add anything to the List except null. 
methodOne(ArrayList<? super x> l): 
If x is a class then this method is applicable for ArrayList of either x type or its super classes. 
If x is an interface then this method is applicable for ArrayList of either x type or super classes of 
implementation class of x. 
But within the method we can add x type objects and null to the List. 
eg: Runnable 
| 
Thread<==super class====== Object -----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Which of the following declarations are allowed? 
1. ArrayList<String> l1=new ArrayList<String>();//valid 
2. ArrayList<?> l2=new ArrayList<String>();//valid 
3. ArrayList<?> l3=new ArrayList<Integer>();/valid 
4. ArrayList<? extends Number> l4=new ArrayList<Integer>();//valid 
5. ArrayList<? extends Number> l5=new ArrayList<String>();//invalid(String and 
Number no relationship) 
6. ArrayList Number>(); //invalid becoz of <? extends 
Number is right hand side> 
7. ArrayList<?> l7=new ArrayList<?>(); //invalid 
Declaring type parameter at method level 
=================================== 
We have to declare just before return type. 
Which of the following declarations are allowed? 
public<T> void methodOne1(T t){} //valid 
public<T extends Number> void methodOne2(T t){}//valid 
public<T extends Number&Comparable> void methodOne3(T t){}//valid 
public<T extends Number&Comparable&Runnable> void methodOne4(T t){}//valid 
public<T extends Number&Thread> void methodOne(T t){}//invalid(2 classes extends not 
possible) 
public<T extends Runnable&Number> void methodOne(T t){}//invalid(first interface not possible) 
public<T extends Number&Runnable> void methodOne(T t){}//valid

Comparable 
In Java, the Comparable interface is used to establish a natural ordering for a class. This means that 
objects of the class can be sorted based on their natural ordering without explicitly providing a 
separate comparator. 
Note : When ever you have to power of sorting the values then you have to Implements the comparator 
interface. 
Interface Declaration: The Comparable interface is a generic interface defined in the java.lang 
package. It consists of a single method, compareTo, which compares the current object with another 
object of the same type. 
public interface Comparable<T> { 
    int compareT  
package Comparator_and_comparable; 
import java.util.*; 
class Student implements Comparable<Student> 
{ 
 int age; 
 String name; 
 String tech; 
 public Student(int age, String name, String tech) { 
  this.age = age; 
  this.name = name; 
  this.tech = tech; 
 } 
 @Override 
 public String toString() 
 { 
  return "Students[ age = "+age+" name = "+name+" Tech = "+tech; 
 } 
 @Override 
  public int compareTo(Student that) 
  { 
  //return this.age-that.age; 
  return this.name.compareTo(that.name); 
  } 
} 
public class Comparables_1 { 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  ArrayList<Student> arr = new ArrayList<Student>(); 
  arr.add(new Student(21,"Teja","Java Stack")); 
  arr.add(new Student(24,"Sai","SQL")); 
  arr.add(new Student(23,"Venkata","Java Spring")); 
  Collections.sort(arr); 
  System.out.println(arr); 
 } 
} 
Out put: 
[Students[ age = 24 name = Sai Tech = SQL, Students[ age = 21 name = Teja 
Tech = Java Stack, Students[ age = 23 name = Venkata Tech = Java Spring] 
Comparator: In Java, the Comparator interface is used to define custom ordering for objects. It's particularly 
useful when you want to sort objects based on criteria other than their natural ordering (e.g., alphabetical 
order for strings, numerical order for numbers). 
Interface Declaration: The Comparator interface is defined in the java.util package. It's a functional  
interface introduced in Java 8, meaning it has only one abstract method: compare(T o1, T o2). 
public interface Comparator<T> { 
    int compare(T o1, T o2); 
    // Other default and static methods... 
} 
Implementing a Comparator: To define custom sorting logic, you need to implement the compare method. 
This method compares two objects and returns an integer value based on their order: 
Return a negative integer if the first object should come before the second. 
Return zero if the two objects are considered equal in terms of sorting. 
Return a positive integer if the first object should come after the second. 
Using Comparators: Comparators are often used with sorting methods provided by the Collections class or 
with arrays. For example, you can use Collections.sort() to sort a list of objects using a custom comparator. 
package Comparator_and_comparable; 
import java.util.*; 
class Students 
{ 
 int age; 
 String name; 
 String tech; 
 public Students(int age, String name, String tech) { 
  this.age = age; 
  this.name = name; 
  this.tech = tech; 
 } 
 @Override 
 public String toString() 
 { 
  return "Students[ age = "+age+" name = "+name+" Tech = "+tech; 
 } 
} 
/* 
 * class ComapartorIMP implements java.util.Comparator<Students> { 
 *  
 * @Override public int compare(Students s1, Students s2) { 
 *  
 * return s1.age-s2.age; } 
 *  
 * } 
 */ 
public class Comparator { 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  ArrayList<Students> arr = new ArrayList<Students>(); 
  arr.add(new Students(21,"Teja","Java Stack")); 
  arr.add(new Students(24,"Sai","SQL")); 
  arr.add(new Students(23,"Venkata","Java Spring")); 
  java.util.Comparator<Students> com = (S1, S2) -> S1.age-S2.age; 
  //Collections.sort(arr,(S1, S2) -> S1.age-S2.age); 
Collections.sort(arr,com); 
  System.out.println(arr); 
 } 
} 
[Students[ age = 21 name = Teja Tech = Java Stack, 
Students[ age = 23 name = Venkata Tech = Java 
Spring, Students[ age = 24 name = Sai Tech = SQL] 
Comparable Comparator 
 Purpose:Comparable: The Comparable interface is used to 
define the natural ordering of objects within a class. It 
allows objects of a class to be compared to each other based 
on their inherent characteristics. Implementing 
Comparable means that the class itself knows how to 
compare its instances. 
 Implementation:Comparable: To use Comparable, the 
class whose objects need to be compared implements the 
Comparable interface and provides an implementation for 
the compareTo method. This method compares the current 
object with another object of the same class and returns an 
integer indicating their relative ordering. 
 Single vs. Multiple Sorting Sequences:Single vs. Multiple 
Sorting Sequences: Comparable: Implementing 
Comparable provides a single natural ordering for objects 
of the class. This natural ordering is intrinsic to the class 
and cannot be changed without modifying the class itself. 
 Usage:Comparable: It's used when the natural ordering of 
objects within a class needs to be defined and when you 
want to leverage built-in sorting methods of the Collections 
framework, such as Collections.sort() and Arrays.sort(). 
 Purpose:Comparator: The Comparator interface is used to 
define custom ordering for objects of a class. It allows you 
to define multiple comparison strategies for objects of a 
class without modifying the class itself. Comparator is 
typically used when you need to sort objects based on 
criteria other than their natural ordering or when comparing 
objects of different classes. 
 Implementation: Comparator: Comparator is a separate 
interface that can be implemented by any class. It provides 
a method compare for comparing two objects of a class. 
You can create multiple Comparator implementations to 
define different sorting criteria for objects of the same class 
or even different classes. 
 Single vs. Multiple Sorting Sequences:Single vs. Multiple 
Sorting Sequences:Comparator: With Comparator, you can 
define multiple sorting sequences for objects of a class. 
You can have different Comparator implementations for 
sorting objects based on different criteria or for comparing 
objects of different classes. 
 Usage: Comparator: It's used when you need to define custom 
sorting logic or when you want to sort objects based on criteria 
other than their natural ordering. Comparator instances can be 
passed to sorting methods to specify the desired sorting order. 
Stream API: 
The Stream API in Java is part of the java.util.stream package introduced in Java 8. It provides a way to perform 
aggregate operations on a sequence of elements, typically collections like lists or arrays. The Stream API allows for 
concise and expressive code when working with collections, enabling functional-style operations on them 
Creating Streams: Streams can be created from various sources such as collections, arrays, or by generating them using 
Stream methods. 
Note: Once you use the strem you cant use it again.if we use multiple times below error will display. 
Here we are takeing the values and putting on the stream and stroring in the strem variable. 
Code Example: 
// Creating a stream from a list 
List<String> list = Arrays.asList("apple", "banana", "orange"); 
Stream<String> streamFromList = list.stream(); 
// Creating a stream from an array 
String[] array = {"apple", "banana", "orange"}; 
Stream<String> streamFromArray = Arrays.stream(array); 
// Creating a stream using Stream.of() 
Stream<String> streamOfElements = Stream.of("apple", "banana", "orange"); 
// Generating a stream using Stream.generate() 
Stream<Double> randomStream = Stream.generate(Math::random); 
Code Example: 
Here we are takeing the values and putting on the stream and printing those values. 
package Stream_Apis; 
import java.util.*; 
import java.util.function.BinaryOperator; 
import java.util.stream.Stream; 
public class Stream_S1 { 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  List<Integer> nums = Arrays.asList(6,5,2,1,8,12,11,10,9,17,18,23,21,22); 
  //By Using annmous Inner class 
  /* 
   * Consumer<Integer> consums = new Consumer<Integer>() { 
   *  
   * @Override public void accept(Integer i) { System.out.println(i); } }; 
   */ 
  // By using Lambda Expression -- > (Integer n)-> System.out.println(n); 
  //Consumer<Integer> consums =  n-> System.out.println(n); 
 //--> 1 Stream<Integer>stream = nums.stream(); 
              //stream.forEach(n-> System.out.println(n)); 
  /* Annomous Inner class 
   * Predicate<Integer> pred = new Predicate<Integer>() { 
   *  
   * @Override public boolean test(Integer n) { if(n%2==1) { return true; } else 
   * return false; } }; 
   */ 
   //Lambda Expression 
   //  Predicate<Integer> pred = n->(n%2==1); 
   //--> 2 Stream<Integer> stream1 =stream.filter(n->(n%2==1)); 
  /* Annomous InnerClass 
   * Function<Integer,Integer> funs = new Function<Integer, Integer>() { public 
   * Integer apply(Integer n) { return n*2; } }; 
   */ 
   // Function<Integer,Integer> funs = (n)-> n*2; Lambda Expression 
 //--> 3 Stream<Integer> stream2 =stream1.map((n)-> n*2); 
    //stream2.forEach(n-> System.out.println(n)); 
  //  BinaryOperator<Integer> Bnyops = (Sum,nextnum) ->Sum+nextnum; Lambda Exp 
  //stream2.reduce(0,(Sum,nextnum) ->Sum+nextnum); 
  // To Get Advantage of Multithreading use parallel stream.nums.stream(). 
    int reslut = nums.parallelStream(). 
           filter(n->(n%2==1)).map(n->n*2). 
           reduce(0,(Sum,nextnum) ->Sum+nextnum); 
    //Filter and Map are Intermediate Functions and Reduce is terimnation function. 
          Intermediate functions will when they are dependent with another function. 
    System.out.println(reslut); 
 } 
}

Date and Time Apis: 
In Java, there are several APIs for working with dates and times. The newer and most recommended one is the 
java.time package introduced in Java 8. Here are some of the main classes and features: 
1. LocalDate, LocalTime, and LocalDateTime 
Represent dates, times, and combined date-time without a timezone. 
Examples: 
java 
LocalDate date = LocalDate.now();  // current date 
LocalTime time = LocalTime.now();  // current time 
LocalDateTime dateTime = LocalDateTime.now();  // current date and time 
Code Example: 
package DateandTimeApi; 
public class DateandTimeApi1 { 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  //To use Date in general operations like printing the date and time 
  java.util.Date utilDate = new java.util.Date(); 
  System.out.println(utilDate); 
  long l = utilDate.getTime();//It is giving the information of utilDate 
 // in milliseconds from 1970 
  System.out.println(l+"ms"); 
  //To use Date in DB operations like insert,update,delete query we use 
 // sqlDate 
  java.sql.Date sqlDate = new java.sql.Date(l); 
  System.out.println(sqlDate); 
 } 
} 
Out put: 
Tue Aug 20 22:18:11 IST 2024 
1724172491934ms 
2024-08-20 
Util Date Sql Date 
* It is a utility class to handles Date in our java program. 
* It represents both Date and Time 
* It is designed class to handle Dates w.r.t DB operations 
* It represents only Date,but not Time. 
Note: In sql package 
Time(C) represents only * Time value 
TimeStamp(C) represents both * Date and Time value 
*Is the parent class from which java.sql.Date is derived. So, 
java.sql.Date is a subclass of java.util.Date. 
Because of this inheritance, you can cast java.sql.Date to 
java.util.Date when needed. 
*Extends java.util.Date, but overrides methods related to 
time to maintain only the date information. 
Difference Between Util Date and Sql Date 
Joda-Time API:  The Joda-Time API was a popular third-party library for handling dates and times 
in Java before the introduction of the java.time package in Java 8. It provided a more intuitive and 
powerful alternative to the traditional java.util.Date and java.util.Calendar classes, which had several 
design issues. 
Until Java 1.7 version the classes present in Java.util package to handle Date andTime (like Date, 
Calendar,TimeZone etc) are not up to the mark with respect to convenience and performance.To 
overcome this problem in the 1.8 version oracle people introduced Joda-Time API.This API developed 
by joda.org and available in Java in the form of "java.time"package. 
Key Features of Joda-Time: 
Clear Separation of Date and Time Concepts: 
Joda-Time separates date, time, and date-time concepts into distinct classes like LocalDate, 
LocalTime, DateTime, and LocalDateTime, similar to what the java.time package now offers. 
Immutability: 
Most of the Joda-Time classes are immutable and thread-safe, which was an improvement over the 
mutable and less thread-safe classes in the standard Java library. 
Better Handling of Time Zones: 
The API offers robust support for time zones through classes like DateTimeZone, which handle time 
zone differences more effectively than the old Java classes. 
Code Example: 
package DateandTimeApi; 
import java.time.LocalDate; 
import java.time.LocalTime; 
public class DataAndTimeAPI2 { 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  LocalDate date = LocalDate.now(); 
  System.out.println("Current Date : "+date); 
  System.out.println("-----------------------"); 
  LocalTime time = LocalTime.now(); 
  System.out.println("Current time : "+time); 
 } 
} 
Out put:  
Current Date : 2024-08-24 ----------------------- 
Current time : 15:34:29.119014700 
Fluent API Design: 
Joda-Time features a fluent API design that makes it easier to read and write code. For example: 
DateTime dateTime = new DateTime() 
    .withYear(2024) 
    .withMonthOfYear(8) 
    .withDayOfMonth(20) 
    .withHourOfDay(15) 
    .withMinuteOfHour(30); 
Comprehensive Date and Time Manipulation: 
Joda-Time provides methods for adding, subtracting, and calculating differences between dates 
and times: 
DateTime tomorrow = dateTime.plusDays(1); 
Period period = new Period(dateTime, tomorrow); 
Code Example: 
package DateandTimeApi; 
import java.time.*; 
public class DataAndTimeAPI2 { 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  LocalDate date = LocalDate.now(); 
  System.out.println("Current Date : "+date); 
  System.out.println("-----------------------"); 
  LocalTime time = LocalTime.now(); 
  System.out.println("Current time : "+time); 
  //Getting the Day in the a Month 
  int dd = date.getDayOfMonth(); 
  int mm = date.getMonthValue(); 
  int yy = date.getYear(); 
  System.out.println("Date - "+dd+" - Month - "+mm+" - Year - "+yy); 
  LocalDateTime dtm = LocalDateTime.of(2001,1,6,12,45); 
  System.out.println("Local Current Time ## : "+dtm); 
  System.out.println("Forwarded Six Months : "+dtm.plusMonths(8)); 
  System.out.println("Backworded six Months : "+dtm.minusMonths(3)); 
 } 
} 
Out put: 
Current Date : 2024-08-24 ----------------------- 
Current time : 17:11:30.938104600 
Date - 24 - Month - 8 - Year - 2024 
Local Current Time ## : 2001-01-06T12:45 
Forwarded Six Months : 2001-09-06T12:45 
Backworded six Months : 2000-10-06T12:45 
ZonedDateTime zonedDateTime = ZonedDateTime.now();  // current date-time with timezone 
OffsetDateTime offsetDateTime = OffsetDateTime.now();  // current date-time with offset 
3. Instant 
Represents a point in time (e.g., a timestamp) in UTC. 
Example: 
Instant instant = Instant.now();  // current timestamp 
4. Period and Duration 
Used for measuring the time between two dates or times. 
Examples: 
java 
Period period = Period.between(LocalDate.of(2020, 1, 1), LocalDate.now()); 
Duration duration = Duration.between(LocalTime.of(12, 0), LocalTime.now()); 
Using the Date Api Getting the Age Details..?  
package DateandTimeApi; 
import java.time.LocalDate; 
import java.time.Period; 
public class CheckingAgeUseingLocalDateApi { 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  LocalDate today =  LocalDate.now(); 
  LocalDate birthday = LocalDate.of(2001,9,4); 
  Period period = Period.between(today, birthday); 
  System.out.printf("Current Age of yours age is %d years %d month %d days " 
    ,period.getYears(), 
    period.getMonths(),period.getDays()); 
 } 
}

years -11 month -21 days  
Verifing the LeapYear Using Date Api….? 
package DateandTimeApi; 
import java.time.Year; 
public class LeapYearDateApi { 
    public static void main(String[] args) { 
        String str = "2024"; 
        int n = Integer.parseInt(str); 
        Year year = Year.of(n); 
        if (year.isLeap()) { 
            System.out.printf("%d is a Leap YEAR%n", year.getValue()); 
        } else { 
            System.out.printf("%d is not a Leap YEAR%n", year.getValue()); 
        } 
    } 
} 
Out put: 
2024 is a Leap YEAR 
Note: 
Date -> LocalDate(C) 
Time -> LocalTime(C) 
Date & Time -> LocalDateTime(c) 
now() --->Current information 
of() --> user specific information 
ZoneId -> Setting up the particular zone to fetch the information 
ZondDateTime-> To get the Date and time information of any zone. 
Period ---> To find difference b/w 2 date Objects 
Year ---> To check whether the supplied year is leapYear or not. 
Enums in Java: 
Enums in Java are a special type of class that represents a group of constants (unchangeable variables, like final 
variables). An enum is short for "enumeration," a type that consists of a set of named values called elements or 
members. 
Key Features of Enums in Java: 
1. Type-Safe: Enums provide a type-safe way to define and use constants. This means you can't assign any 
value other than the predefined enum constants to a variable of the enum type. 
2. Predefined Constants: The constants in an enum are static and final, implicitly. 
3. Enum Class: Each enum is its own class, implicitly extending java.lang.Enum. This means it cannot extend 
any other class, but it can implement interfaces. 
4. Switch Statement: Enums can be used in switch statements, providing a cleaner and more readable way to 
work with conditional logic. 
5. Methods and Constructors: Enums can have fields, methods, and constructors. However, enum constructors 
are implicitly private, so you can't instantiate enums using the new keyword. 
6. Iteration: You can iterate over the constants of an enum using a for-each loop or values() method. 
7. Inside Enums usually we can see mostly as constants, but we can also provide inside Enums like constants, 
fields, methods,methods and constructors as well. 
  5th Nov Live Class Enum, 
Annotations in Java 
By using enums in Java, you can effectively create your own custom data type….? 
Explanation: 
Custom Data Type: 
An enum is a special kind of class that allows you to define a fixed set of constants. These constants are the only 
possible values that a variable of the enum type can have. 
By defining an enum, you're creating a custom data type that can only take on specific predefined values. This is 
especially useful when you want to restrict a variable to have only a limited set of valid values. 
Type Safety: 
Enums provide type safety. For example, if you create an enum called Day, a variable of this type can only hold 
values like Day.MONDAY, Day.TUESDAY, etc. You can't accidentally assign a value outside of this range, which 
reduces the risk of errors in your code. 
Custom Behavior: 
Beyond just representing a set of constants, enums can also include fields, methods, and constructors. This allows 
you to encapsulate related data and behavior within the enum, making it more powerful and expressive than a 
simple set of constants. 
package com.Enums; 
// Define a custom data type using Enum 
enum TrafficLight { 
    RED(30), // Stop for 30 seconds 
    YELLOW(5), // Slow down for 5 seconds 
    GREEN(25); // Go for 25 seconds 
    private int duration; // Duration for each signal in seconds 
    // Constructor to initialize the duration 
    TrafficLight(int duration) { 
        this.duration = duration; 
    } 
    // Method to get the duration of the signal 
    public int getDuration() { 
        return duration; 
    } 
} 
public class TrafficLightTest { 
    public static void main(String[] args) { 
        // Using the custom data type 
        TrafficLight light = TrafficLight.RED; 
        System.out.println("Light: " + light + ", Duration: " + light.getDuration() + " 
seconds"); 
        // Iterate over all traffic light signals 
        for (TrafficLight t : TrafficLight.values()) { 
            System.out.println(t + ": " + t.getDuration() + " seconds"); 
            int index =t.ordinal(); 
            System.out.println("Light : "+t+"Index at : "+index); 
        } 
    } 
} 
Code Example 
Light: RED, Duration: 30 seconds 
RED: 30 seconds 
Light : REDIndex at : 0 
YELLOW: 5 seconds 
Light : YELLOWIndex at : 1 
GREEN: 25 seconds 
Light : GREENIndex at : 2

{ 
 enum Gender 
 { 
  MALE, FEMALE, OTHERS; 
 } 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
         Result r= Result.PASS; 
         System.out.println("Result : "+r); 
         Gender g=Gender.MALE; 
         System.out.println("Gender : "+g); 
         int i=Gender.MALE.ordinal(); 
         System.out.println("Index :"+i); 
 } 
}

package com.Enums; 
enum ExamnationResults1 
{ 
 PASS, FAIL, NR;

  * Result1() { System.out.println("Lets how many times"); } 
  */ 
} 
public class OutComeResults { 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  ExamnationResults1 r=ExamnationResults1.FAIL; 
  //Result1 rr=new Result1(); not possible 
  switch(r) 
  { 
   case PASS: System.out.println("Passed"); 
   break;

   break;

Enum Planet: 
This enum defines eight constants, one for each planet in the solar system. 
Each constant has associated mass and radius values. 
The constructor initializes these values. 
A method surfaceGravity() calculates the surface gravity based on the mass and radius. 
Main Method: 
The main method demonstrates how to use the enum in a switch statement to handle different planets. 
It also shows how to iterate over all the enum values using a for-each loop, printing out each planet's name, mass, radius, and 
calculated surface gravity. 
This output gives detailed information about each planet and shows how enums can be powerful when combined with custom 
fields and methods. 
package com.Enums; 
// Define the enum with custom fields and methods 
enum Planet { 
    MERCURY(3.303e+23, 2.4397e6), 
    VENUS(4.869e+24, 6.0518e6), 
    EARTH(5.976e+24, 6.37814e6), 
    MARS(6.421e+23, 3.3972e6), 
    JUPITER(1.9e+27, 7.1492e7), 
    SATURN(5.688e+26, 6.0268e7), 
    URANUS(8.686e+25, 2.5559e7), 
    NEPTUNE(1.024e+26, 2.4746e7); 
    private final double mass;   // in kilograms 
    private final double radius; // in meters 
    // Constructor to initialize the fields 
    Planet(double mass, double radius) { 
        this.mass = mass; 
        this.radius = radius; 
    } 
    // Method to calculate surface gravity 
    public double surfaceGravity() { 
        final double G = 6.67300E-11;  // universal gravitational constant in m^3 kg^-1 s^-2 
        return G * mass / (radius * radius); 
    } 
    // Getter methods for mass and radius 
    public double mass() { 
        return mass; 
    } 
    public double radius() { 
        return radius; 
    } 
} 
public class EnumExample { 
    public static void main(String[] args) { 
        // Example of using an enum in a switch statement 
        Planet myPlanet = Planet.EARTH; 
        System.out.println("Selected Planet: " + myPlanet); 
        switch (myPlanet) { 
            case MERCURY: 
                System.out.println("Mercury is the closest planet to the Sun."); 
                break; 
            case EARTH: 
                System.out.println("Earth is our home planet."); 
                break; 
            case JUPITER: 
                System.out.println("Jupiter is the largest planet in our solar system."); 
                break; 
            default: 
                System.out.println("It's another planet."); 
                break; 
        } 
        // Example of iterating over enum values 
        System.out.println("\nSurface gravity of planets:"); 
        for (Planet p : Planet.values()) { 
            System.out.printf("Planet: %s, Mass: %.3e kg, Radius: %.3e m, Surface Gravity: %.2f m/s^2%n",  
                              p, p.mass(), p.radius(), p.surfaceGravity()); 
        } 
    } 
}

Annotations in Java: 
Annotations in Java are a form of metadata that provide additional information about the 
code. They do not directly affect the execution of the code but can be used by the compiler, 
tools, or libraries to perform various tasks such as generating code, configuring 
frameworks, or validating code during compilation. 
Key Features of Annotations: 
Metadata: 
Annotations act as metadata, which means they describe something about the code but 
don't change its behavior directly. 
They can be applied to classes, methods, fields, parameters, and other elements of the 
code. 
Compiler Instructions: 
Annotations can give instructions to the compiler. For example, @Override tells the 
compiler that a method is meant to override a method in a superclass. 
Runtime Processing: 
Some annotations can be accessed and processed at runtime using reflection. This is 
common in frameworks like Spring or Hibernate, where annotations are used for 
configuration. 
Custom Annotations: 
Developers can define their own custom annotations to serve specific purposes within 
their applications. 
Commonly Used Built-In Annotations: 
@Override: 
Indicates that a method is overriding a method in a superclass. 
Helps the compiler catch errors, such as misspelled method names or incorrect method signatures. 
@Override 
public String toString() { 
    return "This is a custom toString method."; 
} 
@Deprecated: 
Marks a method, class, or field as deprecated, meaning it should no longer be used. 
The compiler issues a warning if the deprecated element is used. 
@Deprecated 
public void oldMethod() { 
    // This method is deprecated 
} 
@SuppressWarnings: 
Instructs the compiler to suppress specific warnings, such as unused variables or unchecked 
operations. 
@SuppressWarnings("unchecked") 
public void testMethod() { 
    List list = new ArrayList(); // Compiler would normally warn 
about unchecked operations 
} 
@SafeVarargs: 
Indicates that a method with a varargs parameter does not perform potentially unsafe operations on 
its varargs parameter. 
@SafeVarargs 
public final void safeMethod(List<String>... 
stringLists) { 
    // Safe operations with varargs 
} 
@FunctionalInterface: 
Indicates that an interface is intended to be a functional interface, meaning it has exactly one abstract 
method. 
@FunctionalInterface 
interface MyFunction { 
    void execute(); 
} 
Annotations Works Based on the Mechanism of the InterFaces. 
Types Of Annotations: 
Annotations in Java can be categorized into different types based on various criteria, including their 
purpose, where they can be applied, how long they are retained, and their usage in frameworks and 
libraries. 
Here's an overview of the different types of annotations: 
1. Based on Purpose 
A. Marker Annotations 
Definition: Annotations that do not have any elements (methods). They are used to "mark" or flag 
something in the code. 
@Override 
public String toString() { 
return "This is a custom toString method."; 
} 
B. Single-Value Annotations 
Definition: Annotations that have a single element. They provide a more concise syntax when used. 
Example: @SuppressWarnings 
@SuppressWarnings("unchecked") 
public void myMethod() { 
// Method implementation 
} 
C. Full Annotations 
Definition: Annotations that have multiple elements, allowing for more detailed metadata. 
Example: @MyAnnotation 
@MyAnnotation(value = "Example", count = 3) 
public void myMethod() { 
// Method implementation 
} 
2. Based on Target 
Annotations can be targeted at specific elements in the code using the @Target meta-annotation. 
Common targets include: 
A. Class-Level Annotations 
Applies to: Classes, interfaces, enums. 
Example: @Entity (JPA) 
@Entity 
public class User { 
// Class definition 
} 
B. Method-Level Annotations 
Applies to: Methods. 
Example: @Test (JUnit) 
@Test 
public void testMethod() { 
// Test implementation 
} 
C. Field-Level Annotations 
Applies to: Fields (variables inside a class). 
Example: @Id (JPA) 
@Id 
private Long id; 
D. Parameter-Level Annotations 
Applies to: Method parameters. 
Example: @RequestParam (Spring) 
public void processRequest(@RequestParam("id") String id) { 
// Method implementation 
} 
E. Constructor-Level Annotations 
Applies to: Constructors. 
Example: @Inject (Dependency Injection) 
@Inject 
public MyClass(Dependency dep) { 
// Constructor implementation 
} 
3. Based on Retention Policy 
The retention policy determines how long the annotation is retained in the code and where it can be 
accessed. 
A. Source-Level Annotations 
Definition: Annotations that are retained only in the source code and are discarded by the compiler. 
They are not present in the bytecode. 
Example: @SuppressWarnings 
@SuppressWarnings("unchecked") 
public void myMethod() { 
// Method implementation 
} 
B. Class-Level Annotations 
Definition: Annotations that are retained in the bytecode but are not available at runtime. 
Example: @Deprecated 
@Deprecated 
public void oldMethod() { 
// Method implementation 
} 
C. Runtime-Level Annotations 
Definition: Annotations that are retained at runtime and can be accessed through reflection. 
Example: @Entity 
@Entity 
public class User { 
// Class definition 
} 
4. Based on Usage in Frameworks and Libraries 
Annotations are heavily used in various frameworks and libraries for configuration, dependency 
injection, and more. 
A. Java Persistence API (JPA) Annotations 
Purpose: Used for mapping Java objects to database tables. 
Examples: @Entity, @Table, @Id, @Column 
@Entity 
@Table(name = "users") 
public class User { 
@Id 
@GeneratedValue(strategy = GenerationType.IDENTITY) 
private Long id; 
@Column(name = "username") 
private String username; 
} 
B. Spring Framework Annotations 
Purpose: Used for dependency injection, aspect-oriented programming, and configuring Spring components. 
Examples: @Autowired, @Component, @Service, @Controller 
@Service 
public class MyService { 
@Autowired 
private MyRepository repository; 
} 
C. JUnit Testing Annotations 
Purpose: Used for writing unit tests. 
Examples: @Test, @Before, @After, @Ignore 
@Test 
public void testMethod() { 
assertEquals(5, 2 + 3); 
} 
D. Servlet Annotations 
Purpose: Used in web applications to configure servlets, filters, and listeners. 
Examples: @WebServlet, @WebFilter, @WebListener 
@WebServlet("/hello") 
public class HelloServlet extends HttpServlet { 
protected void doGet(HttpServletRequest request, HttpServletResponse response) throws 
ServletException, IOException { 
response.getWriter().write("Hello, World!"); 
} 
} 
5. Meta-Annotations: 
Meta-annotations are annotations that are applied to other annotations to control their behavior. 
@Retention: Specifies how long the annotation should be retained (SOURCE, CLASS, 
RUNTIME). 
@Target: Specifies the elements the annotation can be applied to (TYPE, METHOD, FIELD, etc.). 
@Inherited: Indicates that an annotation on a superclass should be inherited by subclasses. 
@Documented: Indicates that an annotation should be included in the generated JavaDoc. 
Summary 
Annotations in Java can be categorized based on their purpose, target, retention policy, and usage in 
frameworks and libraries. Understanding these categories helps in effectively using annotations in 
different contexts, whether for marking code, configuration, or framework integration. 
Code Example: 
package com.Annotations; 
import java.lang.annotation.*; 
@Target({ElementType.METHOD,ElementType.TYPE,ElementType.FIELD}) 
@Retention(RetentionPolicy.RUNTIME) 
@interface CrickerPlayer { 
 String sportName(); 
 String playerName();// default "Cricket"; 
 int runs();// default 20000; 
} 
//@ specifies compiler that annotation type is being created 
@CrickerPlayer(playerName ="Virat Kholi",sportName = "Cricket Player",runs=24000) 
class Virat 
{ 
 public void disp() 
 { 
  System.out.println("Virat is Indian"); 
 } 
} 
public class Annotation2 { 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  Virat virat = new Virat(); 
  virat.disp(); 
  Class<? extends Virat> c = virat.getClass(); 
  Annotation anot = c.getAnnotation(CrickerPlayer.class); 
  CrickerPlayer cp = (CrickerPlayer)anot; 
  System.out.println("ClassName : "+c); 
  System.out.println("Annotation :"+anot); 
  System.out.println("Cricket Player Annotation : "+cp); 
  System.out.println("Player Name : "+cp.playerName()); 
  System.out.println("Sports Name :"+cp.sportName()); 
  System.out.println("Runs : "+cp.runs()); 
 } 
} 
Out put: 
Virat is Indian 
ClassName : class com.Annotations.Virat 
Annotation :@com.Annotations.CrickerPlayer(playerName="Virat Kholi", 
sportName="Cricket Player", runs=24000) 
Cricket Player Annotation : @com.Annotations.CrickerPlayer(playerName="Virat 
Kholi", sportName="Cricket Player", runs=24000) 
Player Name : Virat Kholi 
Sports Name :Cricket Player 
Runs : 24000 
 Example Code 2: 
package com.Annotations; 
import java.lang.annotation.ElementType; 
import java.lang.annotation.Inherited; 
import java.lang.annotation.Retention; 
import java.lang.annotation.RetentionPolicy; 
import java.lang.annotation.Target; 
import java.lang.annotation.Documented; 
// Define a custom annotation with meta-annotations 
@Inherited // This annotation will be inherited by subclasses 
@Documented // This annotation will be included in Javadocs 
@Retention(RetentionPolicy.RUNTIME) // Available at runtime via reflection 
@Target(ElementType.METHOD) // Can only be applied to methods 
@interface MyCustomAnnotation { 
    String value(); // Element that accepts a string 
} 
// Applying custom annotation to a base class 
class ParentClass { 
    @MyCustomAnnotation(value = "Method in ParentClass") 
    public void parentMethod() { 
        System.out.println("Parent Method"); 
    } 
} 
// Subclass that inherits the annotation 
class ChildClass extends ParentClass { 
} 
public class MetaAnnotationExample { 
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception { 
        // Accessing annotations at runtime using reflection 
        ChildClass child = new ChildClass(); 
        // Fetching the method from the parent class 
        java.lang.reflect.Method method = 
child.getClass().getSuperclass().getMethod("parentMethod"); 
        // Checking if the method has MyCustomAnnotation 
        if (method.isAnnotationPresent(MyCustomAnnotation.class)) { 
            MyCustomAnnotation annotation = 
method.getAnnotation(MyCustomAnnotation.class); 
            System.out.println("Annotation value: " + annotation.value()); 
        } 
        child.parentMethod(); 
    } 
} 
Out put: 
Annotation value: Method in ParentClass 
Parent Method 
Meta-Annotations used: 
@Inherited: The custom annotation @MyCustomAnnotation is inherited by the ChildClass from 
ParentClass. 
@Documented: The annotation will appear in the generated JavaDocs. 
@Retention(RetentionPolicy.RUNTIME): The annotation is retained at runtime and can be 
accessed through reflection. 
@Target(ElementType.METHOD): The custom annotation can only be applied to methods. 
Usage: 
In this example, the annotation @MyCustomAnnotation is applied to a method in the ParentClass. 
The ChildClass inherits the annotation from the parent class due to the @Inherited meta-annotation. 
In the main method, the annotation is accessed using reflection to retrieve its value. 
Inner Classes 
Inner Classes: Some times we can Declare a class inside a another class such type of classes are called as Inner Classes. 
In Java, an inner class is a class that is nested within another class. Inner classes are used to logically group classes that are 
only used in one place, which increases encapsulation and helps to maintain cleaner code by restricting the visibility of the 
inner class to the outer class. There are different types of inner classes in Java, each serving a different purpose: 
1. Non-static Inner Class (Regular Inner Class): 
 This class is associated with an instance of the outer class. 
 It can access the outer class's members, including private ones. 
 To instantiate this class, you need an instance of the outer class. 
 Allocting the named class in side of onther class with out using any modifier like static such type of classes are called 
as normal/Regular Inner class.   
 Inside of a inner class Static boundry will wount come…    
Code Example Case 1:  From the static area of outer class we are accessing instace are of inner class… 
Code Example Case 2: Frome Instace area of outter class we are accessing instance area of inner class.. 
package com.InnerClasses; 
public class RegularclassExample1 { 
 class Inner 
 { 
  public void main1() 
  { 
   System.out.println("This is a Inner class instance 
Method...."); 
  } 
 } 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  System.out.println("This is a main Method...."); 
  RegularclassExample1 outter = new RegularclassExample1(); 
  Inner inner = outter.new Inner(); 
  inner.main1(); 
 } 
} 
Out put: 
This is a main Method.... 
This is a Inner class instance 
Method.... 
package com.InnerClasses; 
public class RegularclassExample2 { 
 class Inner1 
 { 
  public void main1() 
  { 
   System.out.println("This is a Inner1 class instance Method...."); 
  } 
 } 
 public void main2() 
 { 
  Inner1 Inner1 = new Inner1(); 
  Inner1.main1(); 
 } 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  System.out.println("This is a main Method...."); 
  RegularclassExample2 outter = new RegularclassExample2(); 
  outter.main2(); 
 } 
} 
Out put: 
This is a main 
Method.... 
This is a Inner1 
class instance 
Method.... 
Code Example Case 3: From accessing instance area of inner class from one more class…i.e out 
side of outer class.. 
From inner class we can access all members of outer class (both static and non-static, private 
and non private methods and variables) directly. 
Within the inner class "this" always refers current inner class object. To refer current outer 
class object we have to use "outer class name.this". 
package com.InnerClasses; 
class OuterClass { 
    private String outerField = "Outer class field"; 
    class InnerClass { 
        void display() { 
            System.out.println("Accessing: " + outerField +" in Inner class..."); 
        } 
    } 
    void createInnerInstance() { 
        InnerClass inner = new InnerClass(); 
        inner.display(); 
    } 
} 
public class RegularInnerClass { 
    public static void main(String[] args) { 
        OuterClass outer = new OuterClass(); 
        outer.createInnerInstance(); 
    } 
} 
Out put: 
Accessing: Outer class field in Inner 
class... 
package com.AcessingVariblesInnerclass; 
class Outer { 
 int x = 10; 
 static int y = 20; 
 class Inner { 
  public void m1() { 
   System.out.println(x); 
   System.out.println(y); 
  } 
 } 
 public static void main1() { 
  new Outer().new Inner().m1(); 
 } 
} 
public class Outterclass { 
public static void main(String[] args) 
{ 
  Outer out = new Outer(); 
  out.main1(); 
 } 
} 
10 
20 
package com.AcessingVariblesInnerclass;

 int x = 10;

  int y = 100; 
  public void m1() { 
   int z = 1000; 
   System.out.println(x); 
   System.out.println(y); 
   System.out.println(z); 
  } 
 } 
public static void main(String[] args) 
{ 
  new Outer1().new Inner1().m1(); 
 } 
}

Code Example : 
package com.AcessingVariblesInnerclass; 
public class Outer2 { 
 int x = 10; 
 class Inner2 { 
  int x = 100; 
  public void m1() { 
   int x = 1000; 
   System.out.println(x); 
   System.out.println(this.x); 
   System.out.println(Outer2.this.x); 
  } 
 } 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  new Outer2().new Inner2().m1(); 
 } 
} 
Out put: 
1000 
100

Note : But for the inner classes in addition to this the following modifiers also allowed. 
1.private 
2.protected 
3.static 
2. Static Nested Class: 
This class is declared static and behaves like a top-level class but is nested within another class. 
It can only access static members of the outer class. 
You don't need an instance of the outer class to instantiate a static nested class. 
Code Example: 
package com.InnerClasses; 
class OuterClass3 { 
    private static String outerStaticField = "Static field"; 
    static class StaticNestedClass { 
        void display() { 
            System.out.println("Accessing: " + outerStaticField + " In Static 
nested class...."); 
        } 
    } 
} 
public class StaticNestedInnerClass { 
  public static void main(String[] args) { 
         OuterClass3.StaticNestedClass nested = new 
OuterClass3.StaticNestedClass(); 
         nested.display(); 
  } 
} 
Out Put: Accessing: Static field In 
Static nested class.... 
Method Local Inner Class: Sometimes we can declare a class inside a method such type of inner 
classes are called method local inner classes. 
The main objective of method local inner class is to define method specific repeatedly required 
functionality. 
 Method Local inner classes are best suitable to meet nested method requirement. 
 We can access method local inner class only within the method where we declared it.That is 
from outside of the method we can't access. As the scope of methodlocal inner classes is very 
less, this type of inner classes are most rarely used type of inner classes. 
 A class declared within a method or block. 
 It can access local variables of the enclosing method only if they are declared as final or 
effectively final (not modified after initialization). 
Code Example: 
 If we are declaring inner class inside instance method then we can access bothstatic and non 
static members of outer class directly. 
 But if we are declaring inner class inside static method then we can access only static 
members of outer class directly and we can't access instance members directly. 
package MethodLocalInnerClass; 
public class Outer { 
 public void m1() { 
  class Inner { 
   public void sum(int x, int y) { 
    System.out.println("The sum is ::" + (x + y)); 
   } 
  } 
  new Inner().sum(10, 20); 
  new Inner().sum(100, 200); 
  new Inner().sum(1, 2); 
 } 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  new Outer().m1(); 
 } 
} 
Out put: 
The sum is ::30 
The sum is ::300 
The sum is ::3 
package MethodLocalInnerClass; 
public class Outer5 { 
 int x = 10; 
 static int y = 20; 
 public void m1() { 
  class Inner5 { 
   public void m2() { 
    System.out.println("Instance variable of Outer5 class... "+x); 
    System.out.println("Instace Variable of Inner5 class... "+y); 
   } 
  } 
  Inner5 i = new Inner5(); 
  i.m2(); 
 } 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  new Outer5().m1(); 
 } 
} 
Out put: 
Instance variable of Outer5 
class... 10 
Instace Variable of Inner5 class... 
20 
if we declare inner class inside static method then it would result in CE.If we declare methodOne() 
method as static then we will get compile time error saying "non-static variable x cannot be referenced 
from a static context".From method local inner class we can't access local variables of the method in 
which we declared it. But if that local variable is declared as final then we won't get any compile time 
error.

package MethodLocalInnerClass; 
public class Outer7 { 
 int i = 10; 
 static int j = 20; 
 public void m1() { 
  int k = 30; 
  final int l = 40; 
  class Inner7 { 
   public void m2() { 
    System.out.println("Instace variable of Outer7.... "+i); 
    System.out.println("Static variable of Outer7.... "+j); 
    System.out.println("m1() local variable... "+k); 
    System.out.println("Final variable of m1().... "+l); 
   } 
  } 
  Inner7 i = new Inner7(); 
  i.m2(); 
 } 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  new Outer7().m1(); 
 } 
}

Static variable of Outer7.... 20 
m1() local variable... 30

Anonymous Inner Class for an Interface: 
• Anonymous inner classes are commonly used to implement interfaces with a single abstract 
method (functional interfaces). 
• You can create an anonymous inner class by using the new keyword followed by the interface 
name or class name (if implementing an interface) and providing the implementation for the abstract 
method. 
Code Example: 
package Functional_Interface; 
import java.util.Scanner; 
abstract class Ano1 
{ 
 abstract public int add(); 
} 
public class Anonymous_inner2  
{ 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  Ano1 an = new Ano1() 
    { 
         @Override 
         public int add() 
         { 
        Scanner in = new Scanner(System.in); 
        System.out.println("Enter the 1st number to add : "); 
         int a =in.nextInt(); 
         System.out.println("Enter the 2nd number to add : "); 
         int b =in.nextInt(); 
         int c = a+b; 
         System.out.println("The Sum of two numbers are :"+c); 
     return c;  
         } 
   }; 
   an.add(); 
 } 
} 
Enter the 1st number to add :  
23 
Enter the 2nd number to add :  
56 
The Sum of two numbers are :79 
General Class 
1) A general class can extends only one class at a time. 
2) A general class can implement any no. Of interfaces at a time. 
3) A general class can extends a class and can implement an interface 
simultaneously. 
4) In normal Java class we can write constructor because we know name of the class. 
Anonmyous Inner class 
1). Of course anonymous inner class also can extends only one class at a time. 
2) But anonymous inner class can implement only one interface at a time. 
3) But anonymous inner class can extends a class or can implements an interface but not both simultaneously. 
4) But in anonymous inner class we can't write constructor because anonymous inner 
class not having any name of the class. 
Static nested classes 
1. Sometimes we can declare inner classes with static modifier such type of inner classes are called static nested 
classes.  
2. In the case of normal or regular inner classes without existing outer class object there is no chance of existing 
inner class object. i.e., inner class object is always strongly associated with outer class object. 
3. But in the case of static nested class without existing outer class object there may be a chance of existing static 
nested class object. i.e., static nested class object is not strongly associated with outer class object. 
File Io Packages in java 
File Handeling in Java 
In Java, file I/O (Input/Output) operations are essential for handling files, reading and writing data to 
them, and performing other file manipulations. Java provides several packages and classes for 
handling file I/O efficiently. 
Here are the key packages and classes used for file I/O in Java: 
1. java.io Package 
This package provides fundamental classes for file and stream-based input/output. 
File: Represents a file or directory. 
File file = new File("path/to/file.txt"); 
FileReader: Used to read character files. 
FileReader fr = new FileReader("path/to/file.txt"); 
FileWriter: Used to write character files. 
FileWriter fw = new FileWriter("path/to/file.txt"); 
fw.write("Hello, World!"); 
fw.close(); 
BufferedReader: Used to read text from an input stream efficiently, buffering characters for 
efficient reading. 
BufferedReader br = new BufferedReader(new FileReader("path/to/file.txt")); 
String line = br.readLine(); 
BufferedWriter: Used to write text to an output stream, buffering characters for efficient 
writing. 
BufferedWriter bw = new BufferedWriter(new FileWriter("path/to/file.txt")); 
bw.write("Hello, World!"); 
bw.close(); 
FileInputStream and FileOutputStream: These classes are used for reading and writing raw 
bytes, particularly useful for binary data. 
FileInputStream fis = new FileInputStream("path/to/file.txt"); 
FileOutputStream fos = new FileOutputStream("path/to/output.txt"); 
File: 
File f=new File("abc.txt"); 
This line 1st checks whether abc.txt file is already available (or) not, if it is already 
available then "f" simply refers that file.If it is not already available then it won't create 
any physical file justcreates a java File object represents name of the file. 
Example First time of Execution: 
package FilePack; 
import java.io.*; 
public class DemoFile { 
 public static void main(String[] args) 
throws IOException { 
  File f = new 
File("C:\\Users\\HP\\Downloads\\abc.txt"); 
  System.out.println(f.exists());// 
false 
  f.createNewFile(); 
  System.out.println(f.exists());// 
true 
 } 
}

false 
true

Code Example: 
package FilePack; 
import java.io.*; 
public class DemoFile3 { 
    public static void main(String[] args) throws IOException { 
        // File Creation By Directory 
        File dir = new File("C:\\Users\\HP\\Downloads\\FileDir"); 
        // Check if directory was created 
        if (dir.mkdir()) { 
            System.out.println("Directory created: " + dir.getAbsolutePath()); 
        } else { 
            System.out.println("Directory already exists or failed to create."); 
        } 
        File file = new File(dir, "Demo.txt"); 
        // Create the file 
        if (file.createNewFile()) { 
            System.out.println("File created: " + file.getAbsolutePath()); 
        } else { 
            System.out.println("File already exists: " + file.getAbsolutePath()); 
        } 
        // Check if the file exists 
        System.out.println("File exists: " + file.exists()); // should be true if created 
    } 
} 
Out Put: 
Directory already exists or failed to 
create. 
File already exists: 
C:\Users\HP\Downloads\FileDir\Demo.txt 
File exists: true 
 The important methods of the File class in Java that you've listed. The File class is used to represent 
file and directory paths in an abstract way, and it provides various methods to perform operations on 
files and directories. 
boolean exists(): 
Description: This method checks if the file or directory represented by the File object exists on the 
file system. 
Returns: true if the file or directory exists, false otherwise. 
File file = new File("example.txt"); 
if (file.exists()) { 
    System.out.println("File exists."); 
} else { 
    System.out.println("File does not exist."); 
} 
boolean createNewFile() 
Description: This method creates a new file in the file system. If the file already exists, it returns 
false. If the file is created successfully, it returns true. 
Returns: true if the file was successfully created, false if the file already exists or if an I/O error 
occurs. 
Throws: IOException if an error occurs while creating the file. 
boolean mkdir() 
Description: This method creates a directory represented by the File object. The directory will only 
be created if it does not already exist, and if all parent directories exist (i.e., it won't create parent 
directories). 
Returns: true if the directory was successfully created, false if it already exists or if the directory 
couldn't be created (e.g., because the parent directory does not exist).  
boolean isFile() Description: This method checks if the File object represents a regular file (i.e., not 
a directory). It returns true if the file exists and is a file, otherwise it returns false. 
Returns: true if the File object represents a file, false if it doesn't exist or is a directory. 
try { 
    File file = new File("example.txt"); 
    if (file.createNewFile()) { 
        System.out.println("File created successfully."); 
    } else { 
        System.out.println("File already exists."); 
    } 
} catch (IOException e) { 
    e.printStackTrace(); 
} 
File dir = new File("myDirectory"); 
if (dir.mkdir()) { 
    System.out.println("Directory created."); 
} else { 
    System.out.println("Failed to create directory."); 
} 
File file = new File("example.txt"); 
if (file.isFile()) { 
    System.out.println("It is a file."); 
} else { 
    System.out.println("It is not a file."); 
} 
boolean isDirectory() 
Description: This method checks if the File object represents a directory. It returns true if the 
directory exists and the object is a directory. 
Returns: true if the File object is a directory, false otherwise. 
File dir = new File("myDirectory"); 
if (dir.isDirectory()) { 
System.out.println("It is a directory."); 
} else { 
System.out.println("It is not a directory."); 
} 
String[] list() 
Description: This method returns an array of String representing the names of the files and 
directories in the directory represented by the File object. It only works if the File object represents a 
directory. 
Returns: An array of filenames (String[]) if the File object is a directory, null if the object is not a 
directory or if an I/O error occurs. 
File dir = new File("myDirectory"); 
String[] files = dir.list(); 
if (files != null) { 
for (String file : files) { 
System.out.println(file); 
} 
} else { 
System.out.println("Directory is empty or an error occurred."); 
} 
long length() 
Description: This method returns the size of the file in bytes. If the File object is a directory, the 
return value is unspecified. 
Returns: The length (in bytes) of the file represented by the File object, or 0 if the file does not exist 
or if it's a directory. 
File file = new File("example.txt"); 
System.out.println("File size: " + file.length() + " bytes."); 
boolean delete() 
Description: This method deletes the file or directory represented by the File object. If the object 
represents a directory, the directory must be empty for the deletion to succeed. 
Returns: true if the file or directory was successfully deleted, false otherwise. 
File file = new File("example.txt"); 
if (file.delete()) { 
System.out.println("File deleted successfully."); 
} else { 
System.out.println("Failed to delete the file."); 
}

package FilePack; 
import java.io.*; 
public class GetonlyFileDirectoryNames { 
 public static void main(String[] args) { 
  File getFilesFromDir = new File("D:\\Computer langauges"); 
  String [] dirFiles = getFilesFromDir.list(); 
  int dircount =0; 
  int filecount =0; 
  for(String file : dirFiles) 
  { 
   File fileText = new File(getFilesFromDir,file); 
   if(fileText.isDirectory()) 
   { 
    System.out.println("Directory :::: "+file); 
    dircount++; 
   } 
   else if(fileText.isFile()) 
   { 
    System.out.println("File :::: "+file); 
    filecount++; 
   } 
  } 
  System.out.println("Total no of Directory avalible path :: "+dircount); 
  System.out.println("Total no of Directory avalible path :: "+filecount); 
 } 
}

FileReader and FileWriter, which are basic classes in Java used for reading from and writing to text 
files. Both belong to the java.io package and are used for character-based input and output. Here's an 
explanation of each: 
FileWriter 
FileWriter is a class used to write characters to a file. It extends the OutputStreamWriter class, which 
in turn extends the Writer class. 
Key Features of FileWriter: 
Character Stream: It is used for writing character data (not bytes) to a file. 
For Text Files: It is ideal for writing text data to a file (e.g., writing .txt, .csv, or other plain text 
formats). 
Low-Level Writing: It writes directly to the file without buffering. If buffering is needed, it should 
be wrapped with a BufferedWriter. 
Constructors: 
FileWriter fw=new FileWriter(String name); 
FileWriter fw=new FileWriter(File f); 
The above 2 constructors meant for overriding the data to the file.Instead of overriding if we want 
append operation then we should go for the following 2 constructors. 
FileWriter fw=new FileWriter(String name,boolean append); 
FileWriter fw=new FileWriter(File f,boolean append); 
If the specified physical file is not already available then these constructors  
will create that file. 
Methods: 
1. write(int ch) : To write a single character to the file. 
2. write(char[] ch) :To write an array of characters to the file. 
3. write(String s): To write a String to the file. 
4. flush(): To give the guarantee the total data include last character also written to the file. 
5. close(): To close the stream. 
Code Example: 
package FileWriter; 
import java.io.*; 
public class FileWriter1 { 
 public static void main(String[] args) throws IOException { 
  //FileWriter fw = new FileWriter("C:\\Users\\HP\\Downloads\\FileDir\\abc.txt"); 
  // with out over ride the file 
  FileWriter fw = new 
FileWriter("C:\\Users\\HP\\Downloads\\FileDir\\abc.txt",true); 
  fw.write(65); 
  fw.write("\nAkula\nVenkata Sai Teja \nJava\nTrainee"); 
  fw.write("\n"); 
  char ch[] = { '3', '0', '1' }; 
  fw.write(ch); 
  fw.flush(); 
  fw.close(); 
 } 
} 
Note: 
The main problem with FileWriter is we have to insert line separator manually,which is difficult to 
the programmer. ('\n')And even line separator varing from system to system.Represenation of "\n" 
would vary from system to system. 
FileReader 
FileReader is a class used to read the contents of a file in the form of characters. It extends the 
InputStreamReader class, which in turn extends the Reader class. 
Key Features of FileReader: 
Character Stream: It is used to read character files, meaning it reads data one character at a time (not 
byte-by-byte). 
For Text Files: It is typically used to read files that contain text (e.g., .txt, .csv). 
Low-Level File Reading: It reads a file directly from the disk but does not buffer the input. If you 
want buffering for efficiency, you would wrap it with a BufferedReader. 
Constructors: 
FileReader fr=new FileReader(String name);  
FileReader fr=new FileReader (File f); 
Methods: 
1. int read() : It attempts to read next character from the file and return its Unicode value. If the next 
character is not available then we will get -1.  
2. int i=fr.read(); 
3. System.out.println((char)i) As this method returns unicodevalue , while printing we have to perform 
type casting. 
4. int read(char[] ch) It attempts to read enough characters from the file into char[] array and returns 
the no of characters copied from the file into char[] array. 
5. File f=new File("abc.txt"); 
6. Char[] ch=new Char[(int)f.length()]; 
7. void close(); 
Code Example: 
package FileReaderPack; 
import java.io.*; 
public class FileReadDemo { 
 public static void main(String[] args) throws IOException { 
  FileReader fileRead = new 
FileReader("C:\\Users\\HP\\Downloads\\FileDir\\abc.txt"); 
  int i = fileRead.read(); 
  while(i!=-1) 
  { 
   System.out.print((char)i); 
   i= fileRead.read(); 
  } 
  fileRead.close(); 
 } 
}

eg#2. Reading an array of characters: 
Let us assume abc.txt has 1000 characters are available  
Scenario1:  
FileReader fr=new FileReader("abc.txt");  
char[] ch=new char[10];  
int noOfCharactersCopied=fr.read(ch); 
Scenario2: 
FileReader fr=new FileReader("abc.txt"); 
char[] ch=new char[10000];  
int noOfCharactersCopied=fr.read(ch); 
Code Example1: 
package FileReaderPack; 
import java.io.*; 
public class FileReaderDemo1 { 
 public static void main(String[] args) 
throws IOException { 
  File file = new 
File("C:\\Users\\HP\\Downloads\\FileDir\\abc.
txt"); 
  FileReader fileRead = new 
FileReader(file); 
  char arr[] = new 
char[(int)file.length()]; 
  fileRead.read(arr); 
  for(char c:arr) 
  { 
   System.out.print(c); 
  } 
  fileRead.close(); 
 } 
}

Limiatations: 
Usage of FileWriter and FileReader is not recommended because of following reason 
1.While writing data by FileWriter compulsory we should insert line separator(\n) manually which is 
a bigger headache to the programmer. 
2. While reading data by FileReader we have to read character by character insteadof line by line 
which is not convenient to the programmer. Assume we need to search for a 10 digit mobile no present 
in a file called "mobile.txt" Since we can read only character just to search one mobile no 10 searching 
and to search 10,000 mobile no we need to read 1cr times,so performance is very low. 
3. To overcome these limitations we should go for BufferedWriter and BufferedReader concepts. 
BufferedWriter: 
BufferedWriter is a class in Java used to write text to an output stream (such as a file) efficiently. It 
belongs to the java.io package and provides buffering capabilities, which means it writes data in large 
chunks instead of writing character by character. This buffering improves performance, especially when 
dealing with large amounts of data or when making frequent write operations. 
How BufferedWriter Works 
When you use BufferedWriter, the data is first stored in an internal buffer, and when the buffer is full 
(or manually flushed), the data is written to the file or output stream in one go. This reduces the number 
of I/O operations, improving efficiency. 
It is often used when writing text files to ensure efficient file writing. 
It can't communicate with the file directly, it can communicate only with writer Object. 
Constructor  
BufferedWriter bw=new BufferedWriter(Writer w); 
BufferedWriter bw=new BufferedWriter(Writer w,int buffersize) 
Which of the following declarations are valid?  
1. BufferedWriter bw=new BufferedWriter("cricket.txt"); //invalid 
2. BufferedWriter bw=new BufferedWriter (new File("cricket.txt")); //invalid 
3. BufferedWriter bw=new BufferedWriter (new FileWriter("cricket.txt")); //valid 
4. BufferedWriter bw=new BufferedWriter(new BufferedWriter(new FileWriter("crickter.txt"))); //valid 
Methods avalible in the BufferWritter: 
write(String str): Writes a string to the output. 
bw.write("Hello, World!"); 
write(char[] cbuf, int off, int len): Writes a portion of a character array starting from a specified 
offset. 
char[] chars = {'H', 'e', 'l', 'l', 'o'}; 
bw.write(chars, 0, 5);  // Writes "Hello" 
newLine(): Writes a platform-independent newline character to the output stream. This is useful 
because different operating systems use different newline characters (\n on Unix/Linux, \r\n on 
Windows). 
bw.newLine(); 
flush(): Flushes the buffer, forcing any buffered output to be written to the file or output stream. This 
ensures that all the data in the buffer is written before closing the stream. 
bw.flush(); 
close(): Closes the stream and releases any system resources associated with it. It also flushes the 
buffer before closing the stream. 
bw.close();

BufferedReader  
BufferedReader is a class in the java.io package used for reading text from an input stream efficiently. It reads data 
from a character-based input stream (like files) in large chunks (using an internal buffer), reducing the number of 
actual read operations. This makes BufferedReader more efficient than reading each character one by one. 
How BufferedReader Works 
BufferedReader reads text from an underlying stream, like a FileReader, by first reading data into an internal buffer. 
It reduces I/O operations by reading chunks of data instead of reading character by character. This is particularly useful 
when reading large files or text streams. 
You can use it to read text line by line, which is its most common use case. 
Constructors: 
BufferedReader br=new BufferedReader(Reader r); 
BufferedReader br=new BufferedReader(Reader r,int buffersize); 
Common Methods of BufferedReader 
String readLine(): Reads a line of text and returns it as a String. A line is considered terminated by any one of a 
line feed (\n), carriage return (\r), or a carriage return followed by a line feed (\r\n). 
Returns null when the end of the stream is reached. 
String line = br.readLine(); 
int read(): Reads a single character and returns its integer representation. Returns -1 when the end of the stream is 
reached. 
int ch = br.read(); 
int read(char[] cbuf, int off, int len): Reads characters into an array and returns the number of characters read. 
Returns -1 if the end of the stream is reached. 
char[] buffer = new char[100]; 
int numCharsRead = br.read(buffer, 0, 100); 
boolean ready(): Checks if the stream is ready to be read without blocking. 
if (br.ready()) { 
    System.out.println("The stream is ready to be read."); 
} 
void close(): Closes the BufferedReader and releases any resources associated with it. 
br.close(); 
Note  
BufferedReader can not communicate directly with the File it should communicate via some Reader 
object.  
The main advantage of BufferedReader over FileReader is we can read data line by line instead of 
character by character

  String line =bfr.readLine(); 
  while(line!=null) 
  { 
   System.out.println(line); 
   line = bfr.readLine(); 
  } 
  bfr.close(); 
 } 
}

PrintWriter in Java 
PrintWriter is a class in Java used to write formatted text to an output stream. It is part of the java.io package 
and offers several advantages for writing text-based data, such as formatted text, characters, and strings, to 
files or output streams. One of the key features of PrintWriter is that it provides methods for easy and efficient 
printing of primitive data types (such as int, float, double, etc.), objects, and text. 
How PrintWriter Works: 
PrintWriter can be used to write characters, strings, and formatted text to files or other output streams (e.g., 
System.out). 
It is often used for writing text to a file in a convenient way since it provides methods like print() and println() 
similar to System.out. 
It also supports automatic flushing, which can ensure data is written to the destination whenever a newline 
character is encountered. 
Constructors of PrintWriter 
PrintWriter(File file): Writes to the specified file. 
PrintWriter pw = new PrintWriter(new File("output.txt")); 
PrintWriter(String fileName): Writes to the file with the specified name. 
PrintWriter pw = new PrintWriter("output.txt"); 
PrintWriter(Writer out): Wraps a Writer object, such as FileWriter or BufferedWriter, for printing text data. 
PrintWriter pw = new PrintWriter(new FileWriter("output.txt")); 
PrintWriter(OutputStream out): Wraps an output stream like System.out. 
PrintWriter pw = new PrintWriter(System.out); 
PrintWriter(Writer out, boolean autoFlush): Creates a PrintWriter with automatic flushing enabled if 
autoFlush is true. 
PrintWriter pw = new PrintWriter(new FileWriter("output.txt"), true); 
Common Methods of PrintWriter: 
void print(String str): Prints a string to the output stream. 
pw.print("Hello, World!"); 
void println(String str): Prints a string followed by a newline (\n), similar to System.out.println(). 
pw.println("This is a new line."); 
void printf(String format, Object... args): Prints a formatted string (similar to System.out.printf()). 
pw.printf("My age is %d and my height is %.2f meters.", 25, 1.75); 
void write(String str): Writes a string to the output stream. 
pw.write("This is an example."); 
void flush(): Flushes the output stream, forcing any buffered output to be written immediately. 
pw.flush(); 
void close(): Closes the PrintWriter and releases any associated resources. 
pw.close(); 
some Other Methods: 
print(char ch); 
print (int i); 
print (double d); 
print (boolean b); 
print (String s); 
println(char ch); 
println (int i); 
println(double d); 
println(boolean b); 
println(String s);

 }

package Requirements; 
import java.io.*; 
public class Req3 { 
 public static void main(String[] args) throws IOException { 
  PrintWriter pw = new PrintWriter("C:\\Users\\HP\\Downloads\\FileDir\\file3.txt"); 
  // copy from file1.txt to file3.txt 
  BufferedReader br1 = new BufferedReader(new 
FileReader("C:\\Users\\HP\\Downloads\\FileDir\\file1.txt")); 
  String line = br1.readLine(); 
  BufferedReader br2 = null; 
  while (line != null) { 
   boolean isAvailable = false; 
   br2 = new BufferedReader(new 
FileReader("C:\\\\Users\\\\HP\\\\Downloads\\\\FileDir\\\\file2.txt")); 
   String target = br2.readLine(); 
   while (target != null) { 
    if (line.equals(target)) { 
     isAvailable = true; 
     break; 
    } 
    target = br2.readLine(); 
   } 
   if (isAvailable == false) { 
    pw.println(line); 
    pw.flush();// flush to ensure all data is written to the file 
   } 
   line = br1.readLine(); 
  } 
  // closing the resources 
  br1.close(); 
  br2.close(); 
  pw.close(); 
  BufferedReader bfw3 = new BufferedReader(new 
FileReader("C:\\Users\\HP\\Downloads\\FileDir\\file3.txt")); 
  String linee = bfw3.readLine(); 
  while (linee != null) { 
   System.out.println(linee); 
   linee = bfw3.readLine(); 
  } 
  bfw3.close(); 
 } 
}

package Requirements; 
import java.io.*; 
public class Req4 { 
 public static void main(String[] args) throws IOException { 
  PrintWriter pw = new PrintWriter("C:\\Users\\HP\\Downloads\\FileDir\\file3.txt"); 
  // copy from file1.txt to file3.txt 
  BufferedReader br1 = new BufferedReader(new 
FileReader("C:\\Users\\HP\\Downloads\\FileDir\\file1.txt")); 
  String line = br1.readLine(); 
  BufferedReader br2 = null; 
  while (line != null) { 
   boolean isAvailable = false; 
   br2 = new BufferedReader(new 
FileReader("C:\\Users\\HP\\Downloads\\FileDir\\file3.txt")); 
   String target = br2.readLine(); 
   while (target != null) { 
    if (line.equals(target)) { 
     isAvailable = true; 
     break; 
    } 
    target = br2.readLine(); 
   } 
   if (isAvailable == false) { 
    pw.println(line); 
    pw.flush(); 
   } 
   line = br1.readLine(); 
  } 
  // closing the resources 
  br1.close(); 
  br2.close(); 
  pw.close(); 
 } 
} 
ExecutorService : In Java, ExecutorService is part of the java.util.concurrent package and is a framework for managing a pool of threads 
and handling tasks a synchronously. It simplifies thread management by providing methods to submit, execute, and control multiple tasks 
concurrently. Unlike creating and managing threads manually, ExecutorService offers better resource management, control, and scalability in 
multithreaded applications. 
Key Features of ExecutorService 
Thread Pool Management: 
 ExecutorService manages a pool of reusable threads, reducing the overhead of creating and destroying threads for each task.It can 
create a fixed-size pool or a dynamic pool, depending on the workload requirements. 
Task Submission and Execution: 
 You can submit tasks to the ExecutorService using the submit() or execute() methods.submit() returns a Future object, which can be 
used to track the progress of the task or retrieve the result. 
Task Scheduling and Control: 
 Supports scheduled task execution using ScheduledExecutorService, allowing you to delay or periodically execute tasks. 
Graceful Shutdown: 
 Provides methods for gracefully shutting down the executor (shutdown() and shutdownNow()), ensuring that active tasks complete 
or are interrupted as per the application's requirements. 
package ExecutererviceWithFile; 
import java.io.BufferedReader; 
import java.io.FileReader; 
import java.util.ArrayList; 
import java.util.List; 
import java.util.concurrent.ExecutorService; 
import java.util.concurrent.Executors; 
import java.text.SimpleDateFormat; 
public class ExecutorServiceExample {  // Renamed the class to avoid conflict with 
java.util.concurrent.ExecutorService 
    public static void main(String[] args) { 
        List<String> lines = new ArrayList<>(); 
        // Read lines from the file 
        try (BufferedReader br = new BufferedReader(new FileReader("D:\\Java notes and INEURON Daily live 
classes\\IMP PDFS\\13th Nov(Serialization and DeSerialization)\\Serialization and 
DeSerialization\\ExecuterService.txt"))) { 
            String line; 
            while ((line = br.readLine()) != null) { 
                lines.add(line); 
            } 
            System.out.println("Reading Lines Count: " + lines.size()); 
        } catch (Exception e) { 
            e.printStackTrace(); 
        } 
        // Create an ExecutorService with a fixed thread pool 
        ExecutorService executor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(3); 
        // Process each line using the executor 
        for (String strLine : lines) { 
            executor.submit(() -> { 
                try { 
                    // Modify the string as per the requirement 
                    String doj = strLine.replace("Data of joining :##", "Doj :-"); 
                    String dt = strLine.substring(20, 29).trim(); // Ensure you trim whitespace 
                    // Define the date format to parse the string 
                    SimpleDateFormat sdf = new SimpleDateFormat("dd-MM-yyyy");  
                    java.util.Date date = sdf.parse(dt); 
                    java.sql.Date sqlDate = new java.sql.Date(date.getTime());  
                    // Print the result 
                    System.out.println(strLine+" Employee Date of Joining is: " + doj + "  Date: " + 
sqlDate); 
                    Thread.sleep((int)(Math.random()*300)+200); 
                } catch (Exception e) { 
                    e.printStackTrace(); 
                } 
            }); 
        } 
        // Shutdown the executor 
        executor.shutdown(); 
    } 
}
